Choo (Tampines)]</span></td></tr></table></div></div></div></div></div><div _ngcontent-cca-c21="" align="center"><span _ngcontent-cca-c21="" class="normal"><h1 _ngcontent-cca-c21="" class="titleCaps text-center"><strong _ngcontent-cca-c21="">Committee of Supply  Head L (Ministry of the Environment and Water Resources)</strong></h1><!----><br _ngcontent-cca-c21=""><!----></span></div><div _ngcontent-cca-c21="" class="container"><div _ngcontent-cca-c21="" class="col-md-12"><div _ngcontent-cca-c21="" class="panel panel-default"><div _ngcontent-cca-c21="" id="right1" class="panel-body"><div _ngcontent-cca-c21="" id="resizeDiv"><div _ngcontent-cca-c21="" id="showResult"><div _ngcontent-cca-c21="" id="showTopic"><div _ngcontent-cca-c21="" class="hansardContent"><div _ngcontent-cca-c21="" class="reportTable"><p><strong>The Chairman</strong>: Head L, Ministry of the Environment and Water Resources. Er Dr Lee Bee Wah.&nbsp;</p><h6><em>Sustainability and Climate Change</em></h6><p><strong>Er Dr Lee Bee Wah (Nee Soon)</strong>: Mr Chairman, I beg to move, "That the total sum to be allocated for Head L of the Estimates be reduced by $100".</p><p>Climate change is a&nbsp;significant problem. We have witnessed its far-reaching effects in many countries, from droughts and floods to bush fires. We are fortunate that thus far, we have not had to grapple with direct effects of natural disasters caused by climate change. However, being a huge importer of food products, we are at the mercy of the global situation.</p><p>The Government established the Singapore Food Agency (SFA) last year to ensure and secure a supply of safe food and it has done good work to improve sustainability of our food sources.&nbsp;</p><p>Hygiene and food safety are essential to sustainability of food sources. In recent years, there have been several high-profile instances of food hygiene lapses. I would like to ask how has formation of SFA helped to tackle food hygiene issues? How does SFA balance strengthening the food hygiene regime without causing an rise in business <mark class="highlighted-text">cos</mark>t? How does SFA ensure that the food industry continue to build a strong food safety culture even as globalisation and technological advancements transform the food landscape.</p><p>The battle to reduce plastic usage is essential for environmental sustainability. The Ministry is implementing the mandatory packaging reporting framework this year, with the aim to reduce packaging waste including plastics. This is something new to everyone and, certainly, some companies will require more than a little guidance in the initial stages of implementation.</p><p>Does the Ministry have plans to work with the industry to provide support for affected companies? Will the framework be tailored accordingly to different industries? Will it be applied to only local businesses, or also extended to imports, and how will that be implemented?</p><p>Packaging is often used for branding and marketing purposes. Some local businesses will likely proclaim the competition to be unfair if the imported goods may maintain their fancy and more aesthetically appealing packaging with little or no penalty.</p><p>I also note that with the COVID-19 outbreak, many sanitation products were frequently out of stock. Were F&amp;B businesses and places, where food is served or handled, affected by the shortages, and how did they cope with it? How do we ensure enough supplies especially for hygiene in public toilets?</p><p>On the subject of food, food waste remains a significant problem. The Ministry had earlier announced that owners and occupiers of commercial and industrial premises which generate large amounts of food waste will be required to segregate their food waste for treatment from 2024. Can the Ministry share if the scope of affected premises and thresholds have been determined? Are there measures in place to help the industry comply with the requirements? Do hawker centres, coffeeshops, supermarkets fall under this regulation? Will this cause an increase in operation <mark class="highlighted-text">cos</mark>ts?</p><h6>1.30 pm</h6><p>In the last two years, many people were affected by food poisoning incidents as a result of improper hygiene and cleaning practices at places such as pre-schools and food establishments? How do we foster greater responsibility by businesses and society to raise cleanliness and hygiene standards in Singapore?</p><p>Each and every Singaporean must regard environment conservation as our duty. We may be a small dot on the globe, but we are heating up twice as fast as the rest of the world, as a result of the Urban Heat Island (UHI) effect. As an island-city, it is inevitable for us to have many urban surfaces like buildings and roads. Heat generated from our large, high-density population gives rise to UHI effect. We need to reduce the rate at which we are heating up.</p><p>The Government has been working very hard to increase the amount of greenery on our island, which has offered some relief from the heat. I am grateful to NParks for the intensified greening in Singapore in general and Yishun, in particular. With the increase in number of trees and bushes, the area has become a much cooler and more pleasant environment for the residents.&nbsp;</p><p>We are starting to see some new innovative green architecture around the island and I hope some of the existing buildings and infrastructure may soon get a makeover as well. My Nee Soon South CC is being upgraded to become a net-zero energy building. I am very excited about it.&nbsp;</p><p>I hope the Government will set bold national goals, to make it mandatory for high green coverage on new buildings or even to make them net-zero energy or energy-positive buildings. Doing so will stimulate even more innovation and creativity in the building industries. I believe our implementation of photovoltaic (PV) systems could be more extensive.</p><p>I really like what Khoo Teck Puat Hospital has done with their roof tops, which is an energy-saving, green and sustainable combination of community gardens, farms and PV. Moreover, PVs, with their large, flat surfaces, could be designed for multiple functions, such as providing shelters from the sun and rain aside from collecting energy.&nbsp;They could be designed to complement the aesthetics of the buildings they serve.</p><p>Climate change is a crisis but the Chinese term for crisis "" is also made up by two characters, which means danger and opportunity. There are plenty of opportunities if we set our minds to adopting bold changes and strategies. Climate change is now a major concern for many world leaders. If we can set an example and come up with viable solutions to address climate challenges, Singapore would stand among the world leaders in addressing this major crisis, thus securing our relevance in the globe.</p><p>There is clearly a lot of local talent in our country. Since the rise in popularity of green buildings, many of our architects, planners and engineering professionals have risen to the occasion. Their projects combining greenery and unique architecture have captured attention worldwide and even appeal to travellers for their "Instagram-worthy" appearances.</p><p>There are also many ground-up initiatives, ranging from classes on making your own compost, to roundtable discussions, really free markets to exchange and give away unwanted items and so on. Can we pool their expertise together so there is a more coordinated approach and sharing of resources? In Nee Soon South, I intend to turn our under-utilised multi-storey carpark (MSCP) rooftop into high-tech community farm. I would like to ask the Minister: is there any funding available?</p><p>Singaporeans, especially the younger generation, are big on sustainable initiatives. There is also plenty of talent and expertise. The Government must take the lead and strike while the iron is hot. Together, let us make this little red dot a shining example to the world.</p><p>And today is World Engineering Day. I would like to take this opportunity pay tribute to all the engineers, including our MEWR Minister, and his team in MEWR for working very hard and come up with very innovative engineering solutions to make this world a better place to live in. And I am also very proud to be an engineer.</p><p><span class="procText" style="font-family:Arial"> Question proposed.</span></p><p><strong>The Chairman</strong>: Mr Dennis Tan, take your two cuts together.</p><h6><em>Carbon Sinks in Singapore</em></h6><p><strong>Mr Dennis Tan Lip Fong (Non-Constituency Member)</strong>: Mr Chairman, it was reported that land in Singapore changed from a net absorber of carbon in 2012 to a net emitter in 2014.</p><p>According to NParks, this is due largely to land conversion from forests and other vegetated areas to settlements. This is particularly concerning, as over the past few years, we have seen the prioritisation of development projects over the benefits afforded by sensitive eco-systems due to such projects as the Cross Island Line, the Tengah HDB project and the Mandai project.</p><p>Will the Government be committed to securing the inviolability of our nature reserves and greater protection of the little that is left of our more natural green eco-systems in the future? Apart from the forest significant heritage and ecological value, they provide Singapore with carbon sinks, important in our urban environment and helps mitigate the urban island heat effect.</p><p>I also understand that Singapore uses satellite images covering all land use classes and the five carbon pools, as defined by the Inter-governmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) and have established permanent sampling plots across the nation for the tracking of carbon in relevant land use classes.</p><p>I would like to ask the Minister how have or how can such efforts help Singapore improve tree bio-mass growth rates so as to preserve and build more carbon sinks. Will the forest restoration action plan, as announced by NParks in January 2019, help in converting land in Singapore back to a net absorber of carbon?</p><h6><em>Emissions from Oil Refining Industries</em></h6><p>My next cut.&nbsp;Singapore's energy and chemicals industry sector contributes to about 60% of Singapore's total emissions. Around three-quarters of these industry sectors emissions come from the oil refining and petrochemical sector. The Government has set a carbon tax of $5 per tonne of carbon dioxide equivalent for five years and plans to review it by 2023 with the intention of raising the carbon text to between $10 and $15 per tonne of carbon dioxide equivalent by 2030.</p><p>Industrial energy efficiency, particularly for large emitters is currently being addressed by the Energy Conservation Act (ECA). Large emitters are required under the ECA to submit annual energy efficiency improvement plans. From 2021, these emitters must also establish facility-wide energy management systems and conduct energy efficiency opportunities assessments which must be submitted to NEA.</p><p>I would like to ask the Minister how successful has the ECA been in improving industrial energy efficiency for our energy and chemicals industry and more specifically, for our oil refining Industries in the past five years? Can the public have access to aggregated information on the improvements?</p><p>Minister Masago said in his Parliamentary Question reply to me last month that the Government works closely with the oil refining industry to ensure that they achieve high standards of energy efficiency and adopt sustainable practices; and that all three oil refineries in Singapore have set up co-generation plants, which can significantly improve the energy efficiency of the refineries. Has the Government set any projection or targets for emissions reductions for our refining industry in the next five years? And if so, what are these? And if not, will the Government consider doing so?</p><h6><em>Carbon Auditing</em></h6><p><strong>Assoc Prof Daniel Goh Pei Siong (Non-Constituency Member)</strong>:&nbsp;Chairman, Sir, it has been one year since the carbon tax has come into effect and last Friday, Senior Minister Teo announced the goal of capping Singapore's greenhouse gas emissions by 2030, halving it by 2050, and eventually achieving net-zero emissions. I support this goal.</p><p>What we need now is the commitment by Government and businesses to actually reduce emissions and track it. In Australia, carbon neutral certification under the Climate Active Carbon Neutral Standard is an example of how the government supports businesses as they account for and reduce carbon emissions. The Energy Conservation Act currently requires large energy users in the industry and transport sectors to monitor and report energy usage and conduct energy efficiency assessments. A similar framework can be applied to carbon emissions. We should, in the journey to halving greenhouse gas emissions by 2050, require all Government bodies and large companies to conduct annual carbon auditing.</p><p>There are a variety of carbon management services available in the market today. With carbon auditing, organisations can understand their greenhouse inventory, isolate each emission source, and hence, determine and model emission reduction strategies, their payback and viability.</p><p>To help ease organisations into this, the Government could implement carbon auditing subsidies for the next 10 years. If companies successfully reduce their carbon emissions year on year, incremental subsidies can incentivise them to continue to do so.</p><p>Other ways to encourage a greener report card could be doubling on handouts for low-carbon initiatives and progressive carbon tax relief. In 2019, the UK set a new target of achieving net-zero greenhouse gas emissions by 2050, with ambitious policies and generous funding to support this. More has to be done and decisively so, or the climate emergency will continue to worsen.</p><p><strong>Mr Tan Chuan-Jin</strong>: Mr Louis Ng, you can take all your cuts together.</p><h6><em>Promoting the Use of Solar Energy</em></h6><p><strong>Mr Louis Ng Kok Kwang (Nee Soon)</strong>: Sir, the Young PAP held a focus group discussion last month with 16 climate activists and drafted 24 recommendations. These included incentivising solar energy, which is the most viable clean energy source in Singapore.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;In Nee Soon, 64% of the electricity for municipal fittings is from solar energy. We should all move towards being fully solar powered.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;Will MEWR provide incentives to encourage solar deployment on rooftops and also provide consumers with rebates for electricity plans that use solar energy? Will MEWR also deploy more floating solar panels at our reservoirs?&nbsp;</p><h6><em>Bringing Forward the Carbon Tax Review</em></h6><p>Next, climate change is an existential threat. I am glad we have introduced the carbon tax to fight climate change.&nbsp;&nbsp;However, the current $5 per tonne carbon tax rate is too low. The timeline for reviewing the rate is also too long. The Ministry plans to review the tax only by 2023 and increase it to only $10 to $15 by 2030.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;The minimum rate required by 2020 to reach the Paris Agreement temperature target is estimated at $54.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;Young PAP and climate activists hope we bring forward the 2023 review and increase the rate to more than $15 per tonne.</p><h6><em>Releasing Emissions Data</em></h6><p>Next,&nbsp;MEWR has said that it will not disclose the carbon tax contributions of companies because the tax was meant only as a price signal. But a tool can have multiple uses.</p><p>&nbsp;Releasing emissions data by company and facility is not a new idea. The EU publishes the emissions of 15,000 facilities. The UK requires its 2,000 listed companies to declare their emissions. The German Institute of Economic Research has found that such company-level disclosures work, reducing emissions by 17% permanently.</p><p>&nbsp;What I am proposing is a carefully targeted measure. We publish only the data of those that pay the tax. This means only the highest emitters will face pressure. It also means they can stop being published by cutting their emissions. A carrot and a stick.</p><p>&nbsp;MEWR says the Carbon Pricing Act does not allow release of the data. If that is true, we can amend the Act.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;The fight against climate change should be built on transparency. Young PAP and climate activists both agree.&nbsp;Will MEWR study the idea of releasing emissions data on taxable facilities and companies?</p><h6><em>Greening Our Private Passenger Car Fleet</em></h6><p>Next, it is positive news that we have committed to having a fleet of local public buses that runs on cleaner energy by 2040. It is also good news that we intend to phase out internal combustion engines (ICE) and have vehicles run on cleaner energy by 2040.&nbsp;</p><h6>1.45 pm</h6><p>But this will take time as we need to expand the charging infrastructure for electric vehicles.&nbsp;In the meantime, we can steer Singapore towards an environmentally-friendly car society by improving the Vehicular Emissions Scheme (VES). Currently, a hybrid car <mark class="highlighted-text">cos</mark>ts more&nbsp; a lot more. Will the Ministry consider offering higher rebates under the current VES and increasing surcharges for vehicles with higher emissions?&nbsp;</p><p>I understand that we are not in the business of making cars cheaper, but we are in the business of protecting our environment. We can do this by urging people and steering them towards purchasing hybrid cars in the next 10 years, again before the charging infrastructure for electric vehicles is ready.&nbsp;</p><p>It is also good news that we will be extending the VES to light commercial vehicles. Can MEWR provide more details about this?</p><h6><em>Reducing Hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs) Use and Emission</em></h6><p>Next, hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs), commonly used in refrigerators and air-conditioners, are greenhouse gases that contribute to global warming.&nbsp;NEA has put in place licensing controls for the import, export and manufacture of 18 types of HFCs which took effect from 1 January 2019.&nbsp;However, Singapore has not yet ratified the Kigali Amendment to the Montreal Protocol which requires parties to phase-down the production and consumption of HFCs.</p><p>Can the Ministry share whether Singapore will ratify the Kigali Amendment and share its plans to further reduce the use and emission of HFCs in Singapore?&nbsp;</p><h6><em>Reporting Public Service Carbon Use</em></h6><p>Temasek has taken a strong lead in tackling climate change and is urging everyone to join them.&nbsp;As Mdm Ho Ching said a few months ago, "Some businesses have already planned for 100% renewable energy before or by 2050. Others are setting yearly goals to reduce their greenhouse gas emissions. For those of us who have not started, I propose that we take a simple step, to report our electricity and transport usage by next financial year. This is very simple; whether you are big or small, it is a very simple step to take. It is a proxy for our indirect emissions. Once we track and measure, as all businesses know, we can start to scope our solutions to reduce emissions or reduce waste."&nbsp;</p><p>Can the Ministry follow Temasek and take the very simple step of reporting its electricity, paper, travel and water usage?&nbsp;Can the Public Service also follow Temasek and pledge to go carbon-neutral by this year and, like Temasek, ensure that Sustainability is not just one of our roles. It is at the core of everything we do.</p><h6><em>Banning Disposables for Public Service</em></h6><p>Lastly, in Nee Soon East, we have launched our Zero Waste Masterplan. This year, we will expand it to all our RC events.&nbsp;</p><p>We have saved so much. At the same time, it is shocking to me that we have used so much in less than a year. But as a responsible member of the global community, we should do our best to reduce our carbon footprint.&nbsp;Ideally, our cooling appliances should be energy-efficient and operate using refrigerant gas with a lower global warming potential.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p>Many refrigerants have a global warming potential that can be over 1,000 times as potent as carbon dioxide.&nbsp;The Kigali Amendment to the Montreal Protocol aims to reduce hydrofluorocarbon refrigerants by over 80% over the next 30 years.</p><p>I would like to ask to what extent are refrigerators and air-conditioners and other cooling equipment which are both energy-efficient and use refrigerants with low global warming potential available in our market today?&nbsp;How will the Ministry help our consumers make more climate-friendly choices for their household appliances?</p><h6><em>Tackling Climate Change</em></h6><p><strong>Mr Mohamed Irshad (Nominated Member)</strong>: Chairman, I will be focusing on the two funds created in Budget 2020. Researchers projected that 13.6% to 15.2% of global beaches will be lost by 2050. As an island state, sea level rise is an existential issue for Singapore. The Prime Minister shared at the 2019 National Day Rally that the UN currently projects that sea levels will rise by up to one metre by the end of this century, or 80 years from now.</p><p>It was also shared that Singapore would need $100 billion or more over 100 years to build our coastal defences to protect ourselves. It is thus timely that this Budget introduced the $5 billion Coastal and Flood Protection Fund.&nbsp;Can the Minister provide an update and details of the plans to build up our coastal defences?</p><p>And as building coastal defence against sea levels would likely involve multiple agencies and stakeholders, can the Minister also provide details of the Government's strategy to coordinate our coastal protection efforts?&nbsp;</p><h6><em>Flood Resilience and Vector Control</em></h6><p class="ql-align-justify"><strong>Mr Desmond Choo (Tampines)</strong>: Since Singapore's Independence in 1965, we have faced two primary water management challenges  scarcity of fresh water and flood risk.&nbsp;As a low-lying country, we faced the threat and damage of inland flooding. At the same time, we need to manage our scarce water resources well.</p><p class="ql-align-justify">History shows floods have had a long-running effect on Singapore  national records remind us of the extensive floods in Newton Circus in December 1969, Rochor Canal in November 1973 and Braddell Road in December 1978. We have certainly come a very long way.&nbsp;</p><p class="ql-align-justify">From 2013, there have been several flash floods with floodwaters up to half a metre deep. With climate change picking up, Singapore will experience increasingly extreme and unpredictable weather, probably resulting in more frequent and intense storms.&nbsp;What are PUB's future plans to mitigate against inland floods in the face of this challenge?&nbsp;</p><p class="ql-align-justify">PUB has undertaken significant infrastructure developments, including the construction of the Stamford Diversion Canal and the Stamford Detention Tank, to protect the Stamford catchment and the Orchard Road area.&nbsp;There was also a $300 million seven-year-long upgrade of the Bukit Timah First Diversion Canal.&nbsp;Have these helped to prevent floods and strengthen Singapores flood resilience? Are there any more of such major drainage projects that PUB plans to undertake?</p><p class="ql-align-justify">In addition, how do we tackle flood risk and balance this against societal affordability? Could other <mark class="highlighted-text">cos</mark>t-efficient options, including green infrastructure,&nbsp;green areas that retain rainwater and mangrove restoration, be further reviewed? How is the Government also getting the commercial property developers to do their part for flood mitigation?</p><p class="ql-align-justify">Also essential are monitoring and public communication systems that alert populations to flooded areas, such as the ones PUB has developed, to provide early warning of risky, flooded areas.&nbsp;How can we further enhance those systems?</p><p class="ql-align-justify">At the National Day Rally in 2019, Prime Minister Lee mentioned that the sea level rise is an "existential issue". We have committed at least $100 billion to build up coastal defences.&nbsp;The severity and enormity of the challenge requires us to plan way ahead but execute quickly.&nbsp;What is the progress of the Ministrys efforts and what are the concrete measures that have already been embarked upon? When can the public expect to see the overall blueprint?</p><p class="ql-align-justify">&nbsp;Coastal protection will require balancing of varying needs.&nbsp;For example, protecting our coastlines with sea walls and polders might have permanent ecological impact.&nbsp;It might have impact on shipping lanes, underwater cable infrastructure, maritime security and international relations.&nbsp;It is extensive work that cuts across multiple agencies.&nbsp;Would the Ministry consider setting up a one-stop agency to coordinate Coastal Protection work?&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p class="ql-align-justify">Coastal Protection and Flood Mitigation can also bring about significant economic opportunities for Singaporeans.&nbsp;While we learn extensively from the Dutch experts on, for example, polder work, how are we localising such knowledge?&nbsp;Are we also building up a pipeline of local talent in such work so that it might result in another "Water Story" for Singapore?&nbsp;Because of the vast capital needs of such projects, local companies might not be able to participate in this important national project.&nbsp;How is the Ministry ensuring that our local firms can participate and that there is sufficient technology transfer?</p><p class="ql-align-justify">The Government expects to spend more than $100 billion. What principle would be used for financing such projects, noting the need to balance inter-generational needs?</p><p class="ql-align-justify">Lastly, dengue has become a tropical disease that we have to live with.&nbsp;Because of climate change, Singapore is experiencing warmer temperatures.&nbsp;That might worsen the dengue situation. How does this affect our longer term dengue management strategy?</p><h6><em>Dengue</em></h6><p><strong>Ms Sylvia Lim (Aljunied)</strong>:&nbsp;While COVID-19 takes our immediate attention, dengue continues to be a persistent challenge that also deserves significant attention. Indeed, dengue case numbers for the beginning of this year have spiked against the same period last year and there are several live clusters in certain estates in Aljunied GRC. Residents remain concerned even as they appreciate the diligence of NEA teams in the area. The warmer weather in the coming months could lead to an even higher mosquito population.&nbsp;</p><p>In an update in January, NEA has noted the threat from the DENV-3 serotype, a strain of dengue not dominant in more than 30 years. Dengue strains have, in the past, switched from DENV-1 and DENV-2, but NEA has noted a recent increase in DENV-3 cases in three large dengue clusters in Singapore. While NEA has stated that it is still too early to say that there had been a switch of the dominant serotype, low herd immunity towards the new strain will leave Singaporeans vulnerable to a large outbreak.&nbsp;</p><p>In terms of anti-dengue strategy, there was previous discussion about the prospects of a dengue vaccine.&nbsp;However, recent experience from other countries which have tried a vaccine has cast doubts about its efficacy and highlighted potential health risks.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p>Controlling the mosquito population may have better and more immediate prospects. Project Wolbachia is a promising technology for vector control, where infected mosquitoes are deployed for sterile breeding. The project has been expanded in Singapore but it is still under study and is not deployed in existing dengue clusters.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p>Long-term, with climate change and global warming, mosquitoes are poised to breed faster, and the virus replication may also be accelerated.&nbsp;</p><p>In the light of these developments, has the Government adjusted its dengue containment strategies, both short-term and long-term? Also, how has NEA coped with the manpower challenges of such outbreaks and leveraged technology to augment its manpower?&nbsp;</p><h6><em>Vector Control</em></h6><p><strong>The Senior Parliamentary Secretary to the Ministers for Health and Home Affairs (Mr Amrin Amin)</strong>: Warmer temperatures can potentially lead to an increase in mosquito population and spread of vector-borne diseases, including dengue. How will the impact of climate change affect dengue management?</p><p>This year, the number of dengue cases is concerning. There is an increasing number of dengue cases of serotype 3. Singapore needs to be vigilant and be on the alert.&nbsp;Can the Ministry share any new plans for dengue management? How does Project Wolbachia fit into these plans?</p><h6><em>Dengue and Vector Control</em></h6><p><strong>Dr Chia Shi-Lu</strong>: Chairman, even as we battle the COVID-19 outbreak, another health threat is already facing us and it has been facing us for a long while.&nbsp;Since the beginning of the year, the number of dengue infections has been increasing.&nbsp;Last week, NEA reported 110 dengue clusters, of which 25 are at red alert level. Even as I speak, my constituency of Queenstown has three active dengue clusters and one could argue that the dengue problem could pose an even larger and more real threat to Queenstown and to the rest of Singapore than even COVID-19.&nbsp;</p><p>Singapore has been seeing warmer temperatures in recent years as a consequence of global warming, punctuated by more frequent showers, which provide fertile grounds for mosquito breeding.&nbsp;How will the impact of climate change affect our dengue management strategies?&nbsp;What are the Ministrys plans for dengue management in the future and how does Project Wolbachia fit into our national strategy to combat the dengue menace?&nbsp;Will the Ministry please provide an update on the project to date and whether it would be expanded to other areas in Singapore?</p><h6>2.00 pm</h6><p>In addition, I would like to ask whether the Ministry is satisfied with the results of our public education and campaign to prevent mosquito breeding? And how have industries and households responded to enforcement measures?</p><p><strong>The Chairman</strong>: Mr Louis Ng, take your two cuts together.</p><h6><em>Expanding Project Wolbachia</em></h6><p><strong>Mr Louis Ng Kok Kwang</strong>: Sir, Nee Soon East residents have benefited from Project Wolbachia and it has led to a 90% drop in the mosquito population in Nee Soon East. I tell them we are only releasing male mosquitoes and "Males dont bite".</p><p>&nbsp;The next concern is that they tell me the males will attract the females who will then bite them. Singaporeans are not recycling. We have an attitude of convenience and we depend on the Government to do everything.&nbsp;</p><p>Sir, the Government has done what it can to encourage domestic recycling. There is a conspicuous blue bin with simplified images showing what should be recycled at every HDB block and specially marked recycling trucks picking up the recycled thrash regularly. I think it is time we change strategy to turn the National Recycling Programme into community-led recycling.&nbsp;</p><p>Experience in Germany, South Korea and Taiwan, which have the highest rates of municipal recycling, show that when urban communities are mobilised to take ownership of recycling programmes, cultures and norms of recycling become entrenched. In Taiwan, waste collection is not an individual throwaway chore but a community ritual and recycling party where piped music from waste collection truck bring out local residents with bags of recyclables and mixed waste, and community volunteers help residents sort out the thrash properly.&nbsp;</p><p>In Singapore, we have an urban set-up conducive for community recycling efforts. With blue bins located at each HDB block, local grassroots organisations can take ownership of the bins to monitor the recycling trend of each block, diagnose problems such as low rate of recycling or indiscriminate dumping of thrash, and organise targeted events or campaigns to educate the residents of the block.&nbsp;</p><h6><em>Use of Styrofoam Crockery and Products</em></h6><p><strong>Mr Dennis Tan Lip Fong</strong>: Mr Chairman, despite the move towards a greener, zero-waste nation, suppliers of fresh fruits and vegetables, meats and eggs as well as hawkers and cooked food sellers continue to use lots of styrofoam and plastic packaging. Of the 1.6 million tonnes of domestic waste disposed of in 2018, one-third is made up of packaging. More than half of this packaging is made of plastic, but only 4% of plastic waste is recycled.&nbsp;</p><p>In <mark class="highlighted-text"><mark class="highlighted-text">COS</mark></mark> 2018, citing Taiwans ban in single use plastics including straws, cups and shopping bags by 2030, I had asked about the Ministrys plans to reduce the use of plastic disposables in Singapore involving either the restriction or the banning of single use plastic carrier bags, straws and disposable cups, containers and utensils.</p><p>In 2016, the Government also said that it will not ban styrofoam products for food crockery and packaging but will seek to discourage hawkers from using disposable ware.&nbsp;</p><p>We still see much styrofoam food crockery and packaging being used at hawker centres, coffee shops and other food outlets.&nbsp;I would like to ask for an update of the rate of use of styrofoam products in food crockery and packaging vis--vis other materials since 2016.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p>Given their harmful effects on the environment, I would also like to ask whether the Government will conduct a review of the use of styrofoam products for food crockery and packaging use, and whether it will set any target to ban such products or if not, what measures it will take to either reduce or to disincentivise such uses in the next five years. Using fewer plastic bags alone will not solve climate change or save the earth, but every bit counts. And making us conscious of our habits, and consider better alternatives, has value in itself."</p><p>&nbsp;I agree with Prime Minister. I have been calling for a plastic bag charge since 2018. Countries around the world have introduced a plastic bag charge, and it works. In England, there was a 90% reduction. It was estimated that every person was using 10 bags in the most recent year, compared with 140 bags in 2014 before the charge was introduced.</p><p>&nbsp;For a start and similar to England, will MEWR consider introducing a single-use bag charge and make it mandatory for large retailers and optional for smaller businesses and waive the charge for single-use bags for fresh food and meat items? My following set of questions relates to the reflection of these priorities in Budget&nbsp;2020.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;Firstly, allow me to focus on our implementation of Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR) schemes. Can the Ministry share the progress on this front? Namely:&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;One, how many operators have applied for a license thus far and how many have been licensed?&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;Two, do we have sufficient local recycling capabilities to treat the expected volume collected e-waste? What should the industry be expecting?&nbsp;</p><p>Next, on the topic of packaging waste, I note that the Mandatory Packaging Reporting Framework will kick in on 1 July. What sort of support for affected companies will the Ministry be providing to ensure accurate data? Additionally, the framework merely requires reporting, even if they cross prescribed thresholds. Are there any plans and funds for the Ministry to work with the producers to implement their 3R plans?&nbsp;</p><p>Lastly, on the topic of food waste, the Ministry previously announced that owners and occupiers of commercial and industrial premises which generate large amounts of food waste will be required to segregate their food waste for treatment from 2024. Can the Ministry share if the scope of affected premises and thresholds have been determined? Are there measures in place to help the industry comply with the requirements?&nbsp;</p><h6>2.15 pm</h6><p><strong>The Chairman</strong>: Dr Chia Shi-Lu, please take all your three cuts together.</p><h6><em>Managing Packaging and Plastic Waste</em></h6><p><strong>Dr Chia Shi-Lu</strong>: Mr Chairman, packaging forms a significant portion of the waste that is generated by domestic sources, which include households and trade premises, such as shophouses.&nbsp;Statistics from NEA show that one-third of our waste is packaging waste and 55% of that packaging waste is plastic. Considering that only 4% of our plastic waste is recycled, there is certainly potential to further reduce packaging and plastic waste.</p><p>The Ministry had earlier announced plans to put in place an Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR) framework to deal with packaging waste, including plastics, no later than 2025 or, perhaps, even earlier.&nbsp;Under the EPR approach, producers bear the responsibility for the collection and treatment of their products when they reach end-of-life. Would the Ministry share more details about how NEA intends to implement and enforce these plans? Next cut.</p><h6><em>Responsible E-waste Disposal</em></h6><p>I am very happy with the Resource Sustainability Act which was passed last year. It is, I feel, one of the most important pieces of legislation introduced to deal with the increasing amounts of e-waste, food waste and packaging waste, including plastics, due to their quantities and low recycling rates.</p><p>The Act will give effect to the EPR scheme for e-waste.&nbsp;The Ministry announced that a Producer Responsibility Scheme (PRS) Operator will be announced to collect and ensure proper treatment of regulated electrical and electronic consumer products on behalf of the producers.&nbsp;For non-consumer products, producers will be required to provide free take-back of all end-of-life commercial, industrial and electrical equipment before consigning them to licensed waste collectors or e-waste recyclers. With the introduction of the Resource Sustainability Act last year, the EPR scheme will commence and a PRS operator I alluded to earlier will be appointed to collect and ensure proper disposal of electrical and electronic consumer products.&nbsp;For non-consummable products, producers will have to take back and transfer the equipment to licensed waste collectors or e-waste recyclers.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p>Singapore generates about 60,000 tonnes of e-waste a year and the amount is expected to grow year on year. From Town Councils to offices, disinfection and constant cleaning has become the norm. This crisis affords us the opportunity to also reinforce the importance of social norms, such as in our hawker centres.</p><p>For a number of years now, we have been promoting the concept of tray returns. Gradually, we have seen better response. Yet, we also see many previous innovations fall into disuse. In many older hawker centres, you see automated tray-return belts that have been switched off; coin-return machines that do not work; tray-return stations becoming manual stacking stations for cleaners.</p><p>At the same time, the role of the tray is also very much temporary. Many remove their trays out of habit and place dishes onto tables. There is also a socially irresponsible behavior, such as the spitting of food waste and bones onto table surfaces, not just during COVID-19, but on any other day. This should be discouraged. Cleaners often do not have the adequate tools nor training to properly clean. Often, the same cloth is used. And one does not need to leave much to the imagination that the same food waste spat out onto tables ends up being smeared across the entire table.</p><p>I, therefore, ask that MEWR and NEA promote the usage of trays while dining and not remove the tray itself. This way, the tray catches any spillage as well as any food waste or oral fluids. This means that conservancy contractors should also be incentivised to sterilise trays after each use. This reduces the workload of cleaners, promotes a clean hygienic eating environment and reduces the spread of diseases.</p><p>To further achieve this, the design of hawker centres will also have to adapt. Some older hawker centres still use round tables, which are not conducive for the use of trays unlike rectangular tables in newer centres. Beyond COVID-19, I hope that this will promote better hygiene and social norms in Singapore.</p><h6><em>Socially-Conscious Enterprise Hawker Centre (SEHC) Model</em></h6><p><strong>Mr Amrin Amin</strong>: In late 2018, the new management model of hawker centres came under public scrutiny. I was one of those infected as well. There were also 20 deaths, unfortunately.&nbsp;Dengue is endemic in Singapore because of the presence of Aedes mosquitoes, which are the vectors for dengue virus transmission.&nbsp;</p><p>Hence, our strategy has very much been focused on vector control, in essence, to prevent Aedes mosquito from breeding as far as possible. This is a long-drawn battle and it requires constant vigilance on the part of everyone. Our environmental condition, especially our warm weather, also makes it conducive for such transmission. Singapore has seen warmer weathers in recent years, especially in 2019. It was, I understand, 0.9C higher than the past average.&nbsp;</p><p>Could the relatively warmer weather be the possible reasons for the surge in dengue cases in recent years?&nbsp;And if that is the case, how will the impact of climate change affect our management of dengue prevention?</p><p>Recently, Project Wolbachia was rolled out to help reduce the dengue mosquito population. I commend the NEA for pushing the envelope and leveraging new technologies to tackle this serious threat to our public health. Many of these skills might not be supported by existing education pathways.&nbsp;Furthermore, these are not traditional fields for Singaporean students.&nbsp;How does the Ministry plan to jumpstart and develop this field in our Institutes of Higher Learning?&nbsp;One way is perhaps to attract firms to set up their R&amp;D facilities in Singapore to take advantage of our robust IP framework.&nbsp;</p><p>As we move into more recycling, traditional waste management and recycling firms will need to transform.&nbsp;Will the Ministry be able to share what are the new jobs that might be created? Opportunities can include R&amp;D, production, financing and entrepreneurship. How is the Ministry supporting job re-design and helping these workers to find higher-value work in their current industries?</p><h6>2.30 pm</h6><p><strong>The Chairman</strong>: Minister&nbsp;Masagos Zulkifli.</p><p><strong>The Minister for the Environment and Water Resources (Mr Masagos Zulkifli B M M)</strong>: Mr Chairman, firstly, let me wish Er Dr Lee Bee Wah and all the engineers in the House today, Happy Engineer's Day.&nbsp;</p><p>The United Nations (UN) has rightfully identified climate change as the defining issue of our time.&nbsp;For Singapore, like many small nations,&nbsp;climate change is an existential challenge. We feel its effects today,&nbsp;such as in rising temperatures and&nbsp;frequent extreme weather events.&nbsp;Even the seasonality of Singapore's rainy and dry months is now difficult to predict.</p><p>Scientists in the&nbsp;Centre for Climate Research Singapore have projected that by 2100, end of the century,&nbsp;the mean sea level around Singapore may rise up to one metre.&nbsp;Coupled with the frequent and&nbsp;heavy storms also brought about by climate change, low-lying areas of Singapore such as the East Coast may experience more flooding.</p><p>For all countries,&nbsp;climate change also threatens access to&nbsp;essential resources like water, food and energy. These affect the security of our nation too.</p><p>With this said,&nbsp;our history of overcoming challenges gives me the confidence that we need not despair when facing climate change.&nbsp;We will create innovative solutions for the hardest challenge,&nbsp;putting Singapore in the unique position we enjoy today.&nbsp;This is Singapore's DNA.</p><p>Today,&nbsp;I will update on our plans in three key areas to address the challenges of climate change&nbsp; first, mitigation strategies to reduce&nbsp;the emissions of greenhouse gases or GHGs; second,&nbsp;adaptation plans;&nbsp;and third,&nbsp;how we can work together,&nbsp;in our communities and as a nation,&nbsp;to collectively realise a more sustainable Singapore.</p><p>With your permission, Mr Chairman,&nbsp;may I request the Clerks to distribute a document to Members?</p><p><strong>The Chairman</strong>: Yes, please. [<em>A handout was distributed to hon Members.</em>]</p><p><strong>Mr Masagos Zulkifli B M M</strong>:&nbsp;Thank you.&nbsp;First,&nbsp;on mitigation.&nbsp;The UN Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC) and&nbsp;the Paris Agreement are&nbsp;pivotal multilateral agreements&nbsp;to address the global challenge of climate change. It has spurred many countries,&nbsp;including those who are large emitters,&nbsp;to enact laws and transform their economies to meet their carbon commitments.&nbsp;It is therefore in the interest of small island states like Singapore, to support&nbsp;this multilateral framework of cooperation.</p><p>For this framework to stand, every country, large or small,&nbsp;must do its share to reduce GHG emissions. Singapore will also do our part.</p><p>Last Friday,&nbsp;Senior Minister Teo Chee Hean announced that Singapore will enhance&nbsp;our 2030 nationally determined contribution to the Paris Agreement later this year.&nbsp;We will also submit&nbsp;our Long-Term Low Emissions Development Strategy,&nbsp;which aspires to halve emissions from our peak to 33 million metric tonnes of carbon dioxide equivalent (MTCO2e) by 2050,&nbsp;with a view to achieve net zero emissions as soon as possible in the second half of the century.</p><p>Halving our emissions,&nbsp;even 30 years from now, is an ambitious target,&nbsp;because Singapore is naturally constrained in alternative energy. This is an attribute recognised even by the UNFCCC.&nbsp;But we will continue to raise our ambitions to limit and reduce carbon emissions over the long term, knowing well enough that it requires transformational change.</p><p>We are already ahead of the pack&nbsp;by implementing the carbon tax last year  the first country in Southeast Asia to do so. This is a clear signal to the whole of industry that Singapore is moving towards a low-carbon economy,&nbsp;as we give no exemption.</p><p>Mr Louis Ng and Mr Daniel Goh asked about the carbon tax and emissions reporting.&nbsp;The carbon tax, as I mentioned in the past Parliamentary Question, is meant to provide an economy-wide price signal. Hence, only aggregated information will be released. To achieve emissions reductions,&nbsp;rather than scrutinising individual companies, it would be more useful to know which sectors we should concentrate on.&nbsp;The release of individual data could also be commercially sensitive.</p><p>What are the major moves that my Ministry&nbsp;will introduce to further reduce our GHG emissions?&nbsp;</p><p>First,&nbsp;we will implement a package of comprehensive measures to reduce the emission of hydrofluorocarbons, or HFCs.&nbsp;As Dr Chia Shi-Lu and Mr Louis Ng have pointed out,&nbsp;HFCs can be found as refrigerants in refrigeration and air-conditioning equipment,&nbsp;and could leak during installation,&nbsp;maintenance and disposal.&nbsp;Some forms of HFCs trap a much larger amount of heat in the atmosphere than carbon dioxide.</p><p>Later this year,&nbsp;NEA will launch a training course to train and&nbsp;certify technicians to handle refrigerants properly. From next year,&nbsp;NEA will mandate the proper recovery,&nbsp;reclamation and destruction of spent refrigerants.</p><p>With your permission, Mr Chairman,&nbsp;may I display some slides on the screen?</p><p><strong>The Chairman</strong>: Yes, please. [<em>Slides were shown to hon Members.</em>]</p><p><strong>Mr Masagos Zulkifli B M M</strong>:&nbsp;NEA will also introduce&nbsp;a voluntary climate-friendly label&nbsp;to help households identify refrigerators and&nbsp;air-conditioners that use alternative refrigerants with lower global warming potential.&nbsp;In addition,&nbsp;NEA will launch a new grant to support companies who want to make an early switch to&nbsp;more climate-friendly commercial water-cooled chillers.&nbsp;From 2022,&nbsp;we intend to restrict the supply of commercial water-cooled chillers and household refrigerators and air-conditioners that use HFCs&nbsp;with high global warming potential.</p><p>To Mr Louis Ng's&nbsp;question,&nbsp;MEWR will initiate a review on our ratification of the Kigali Amendment to the Montreal Protocol later this year.</p><p>Besides HFCs,&nbsp;improving energy efficiency will have a major impact on emissions reduction.</p><p>Mr Dennis Tan asked about our progress.&nbsp;We have set an ambitious target to improve&nbsp;our industrial energy efficiency to 1% to 2% every year. Our industries are on track to meet this target,&nbsp;and we will continue to improve our energy efficiency rates.</p><p>Last year,&nbsp;NEA announced the Minimum Energy Efficiency Standards, or MEES,&nbsp;for industrial-chilled water cooling systems.&nbsp;MEES will reduce energy consumption&nbsp;in industrial facilities by at least 245 gigawatt hours (GWh) annually&nbsp; equivalent to taking more than 20,000 cars off the road.</p><p>From 2021,&nbsp;energy-intensive companies, including oil-refining companies,&nbsp;will be required to establish facility-wide energy management systems and conduct energy efficiency opportunities assessments.</p><p>In MEWR,&nbsp;we also live&nbsp;by our energy efficiency creed. Let me illustrate this.</p><p>We are on track to complete the development of Tuas Nexus by 2027.&nbsp;The Tuas Nexus carries out&nbsp;co-digestion of food waste and used water sludge to generate up to three times more biogas&nbsp;than conventional sludge treatment processes.&nbsp;This biogas will be used to generate electricity to power the plant and excess electricity will be exported back into the&nbsp;grid.</p><p>In addition,&nbsp;we will ramp up the generation of solar energy within MEWR.</p><p>Mr Louis Ng asked if we can deploy more solar&nbsp;panels on reservoirs.&nbsp;This year,&nbsp;PUB will deploy two 1.5 mega-watt peak (MWp)&nbsp;floating solar systems at Bedok and Lower Seletar Reservoirs.&nbsp;Next year,&nbsp;PUB will deploy the world's largest single floating solar system&nbsp;of 60 mega-watt peak at Tengeh Reservoir.&nbsp;The power generated from this system alone is sufficient to meet the energy of all our water treatment plants at local reservoirs. With this,&nbsp;Singapore's waterworks will be one of the few in the world to be 100% green.&nbsp;</p><p>PUB is also making strides towards improving the energy efficiency of NEWater and desalinated water production.&nbsp;</p><p>Mr Louis Ng also asked if the Government&nbsp;can provide consumers with rebates for using solar energy.&nbsp;Subsidies, such as rebates, distort the energy markets.&nbsp;Pricing energy right will encourage the growth of renewable energy in a commercially viable manner and&nbsp;incentivise consumers to use electricity efficiently, avoiding wasteful consumption.</p><p>Instead of rebates,&nbsp;the Government has streamlined regulations and funded research to support higher deployment of solar.</p><p>My Ministry also intends to be exemplary in optimising the water-energy-waste nexus. We will set out to generate sufficient energy from waste incineration and&nbsp;solar photovoltaic systems to power all of my Ministry's needs,&nbsp;especially in the production of water and the projected production of food.</p><p>In the long run,&nbsp;it is only with groundbreaking technology&nbsp;that we can make huge leaps in climate mitigation. In the horizon are carbon capture, utilisation and storage technologies, and&nbsp;the use of hydrogen as an alternative fuel. We will stay abreast of these developments.</p><p>Beyond the Ministry,&nbsp;the Public sector as a whole is doing more. Mr Louis Ng asked about our efforts.</p><p>In 2017,&nbsp;we launched the Public Sector Sustainability Plan, which charted out our strategies&nbsp;to reduce electricity and water consumption,&nbsp;and achieve Green Mark standards for 100% of our buildings. Ministries and agencies are working hard,&nbsp;and are on track to meet our 2020 targets.</p><p>For example,&nbsp;all air-conditioned Government premises&nbsp;should maintain indoor temperatures of 24 degrees centigrade or higher where feasible.&nbsp;All Ministries have also taken the climate action pledge and committed to reducing their plastic, water and electricity consumption.&nbsp;We are reviewing how to bring our plan to the next level.</p><p>The&nbsp;Deputy Prime Minister also announced our vision for all vehicles to run on cleaner energy by 2040.&nbsp;The Government will take the lead.&nbsp;From 2023,&nbsp;all new vehicle purchases for Government-owned fleets will be cleaner and greener where feasible. MINDEF, for example, will replace&nbsp;400 administrative vehicles with hybrids and&nbsp;later, with electric ones when charging infrastructure is ready in 2040.</p><p>Together,&nbsp;with net-zero buildings,&nbsp;food waste management, our Army is "green" in more than in their uniforms.</p><p>This are but a few examples of what I said earlier in my speech on how the UN Climate Change Framework is shaping&nbsp;climate-conscious behaviours and decisions.&nbsp;We are also reviewing other measures such as the better management of carbon emissions and the use of disposables.&nbsp;More details will be announced later this year.</p><p>Mr Chairman, public transport generates up to three times less carbon dioxide than private transport. Hence,&nbsp;our approach is to encourage public transport and&nbsp;green public transport as far as possible. The Land Transport Master Plan 2040 by the Ministry of Transport (MOT) supports this.</p><p>This is how Singapore moves to cut GHG emissions  simply drive less.&nbsp;If driving less is, somehow, not an option,&nbsp;motorists can still practise sustainable consumption&nbsp;by choosing cleaner vehicles that emit less GHG and harmful pollutants.</p><p>Mr Louis Ng asked about the Vehicular Emissions Scheme (VES).&nbsp;The VES implemented in 2018&nbsp;has seen positive results and&nbsp;we have since extended the VES until end 2020. This will complement the enhanced incentives and infrastructure to promote the use of&nbsp;electric vehicles announced at the Budget. We aim to refine VES further,&nbsp;taking into account its impact on motorists' purchasing decisions and advancements in technology.</p><p>The transport of goods and&nbsp;services is a major economic activity in Singapore. Commercial vehicles,&nbsp;especially Light Goods Vehicles or LGVs,&nbsp;are key emission sources and pollute our air&nbsp;due to their high mileage and reliance on diesel.&nbsp;To support the shift to cleaner commercial vehicles, we will introduce a new&nbsp;Commercial Vehicle Emissions Scheme (CVES)&nbsp;for new LGVs,&nbsp;which form the largest proportion of commercial vehicles.</p><p>Under the new CVES,&nbsp;the cleanest LGVs will receive a $30,000 incentive, while the most polluting LGVs will incur a&nbsp;$10,000 surcharge.&nbsp;In terms of Total <mark style="color:black; background-color:yellow;" class="highlighted-text">Cos</mark>t of Ownership, fleet owners will enjoy overall savings when they purchase EVs as opposed to petrol vehicles and&nbsp;even greater savings as compared to diesel vehicles.</p><h6>2.45 pm</h6><p>To complement the CVES,&nbsp;we will enhance the Early Turnover Scheme (ETS) from 1 April 2021 until 31 March 2023, including expanding the scheme to cover Euro 4 diesel commercial vehicles.&nbsp;The existing ETS will be extended until 31 March&nbsp;2021.</p><p>I have outlined our climate mitigation efforts.&nbsp;Let me now talk about how we will adapt to climate change.</p><p>Climate change will bring about more frequent and intense storms,&nbsp;which may result in major flooding, especially in low-lying parts of Singapore.&nbsp;Mr Desmond&nbsp;Choo&nbsp;asked&nbsp;about PUB's&nbsp;plans to&nbsp;mitigate inland floods.</p><p>Since 2011, PUB has spent almost $2 billion on drainage works, including major projects like the&nbsp;Bukit Timah Diversion Canal and Stamford Detention Tank.&nbsp;We will invest another $190 million, with six projects commencing this year,&nbsp;including the construction of a detention tank&nbsp;underneath Syed Alwi Road,&nbsp;which will enhance flood protection in the Little India area.&nbsp;Our investments will be complemented with developments in technology,&nbsp;such as short-range radars,&nbsp;to better predict and manage heavier rainfall.</p><p>These efforts, however, will not be sufficient with climate change.&nbsp;With climate change,&nbsp;sea levels will rise faster than its natural cycle&nbsp;and along with heavy rains,&nbsp;will overwhelm drainage systems.</p><p>Miss Cheng Li Hui and Mr Desmond Choo asked about our whole-of-nation strategy to coordinate coastal protection efforts. I am pleased to announce that PUB will assume&nbsp;the role of the national Coastal Protection Agency from April.</p><p>With this new responsibility, one agency, PUB,&nbsp;will study both coastal and&nbsp;inland flooding holistically and&nbsp;develop models to guide our flood protection response.</p><p>The investments to undertake coastal and flood protection could <mark class="highlighted-text">cos</mark>t&nbsp;$100 billion over the next 50 to 100 years.&nbsp;As announced by Deputy Prime Minister in the Budget speech, a Coastal and Flood Protection Fund&nbsp;will be set up within PUB,&nbsp;with an initial funding of $5 billion.&nbsp;The fund will be used in a fiscally sustainable manner&nbsp;to support the substantial capital outlay.</p><p>Mr Mohamed Irshad asked about the immediate investments that will be made.&nbsp;Our near-term efforts will focus&nbsp;on studying our coastal areas to ascertain the type, feasibility and extent of measures required.&nbsp;We will phase efforts and&nbsp;start with the City to East Coast stretch and Jurong Island.&nbsp;The funds will also be used to expand and improve our drainage infrastructure&nbsp;to improve flood resilience.</p><p>As we embark on coastal protection, we will also identify new opportunities for urban development.&nbsp;For instance,&nbsp;we could reclaim a series of islands offshore, and even connect these islands up&nbsp;by building barrages to create community spaces for Singaporeans and&nbsp;contribute to water resilience.&nbsp;PUB will partner various stakeholders to explore these possibilities.</p><p>Mr Dennis Tan highlighted the importance of preserving and building our carbon sinks.&nbsp;We will preserve our green carbon sinks and identify nature-based solutions.&nbsp;For example,&nbsp;planting mangroves can both stabilise our coastline by preventing erosion,&nbsp;while preserving our green lungs and supporting biodiversity.&nbsp;Minister Desmond Lee will elaborate in his speech&nbsp;on Singapore's greening efforts.</p><p>Adaptation efforts also involve securing our water supply.&nbsp;Singapore has developed a robust and diversified supply of water,&nbsp;as a result of our early and&nbsp;consistent investments in water infrastructure and research. Along with local catchment and imported water, we will continue to augment our water supply with weather-resilient sources,&nbsp;namely, NEWater and desalinated water.</p><p>With the completion of Marina East and Jurong Island desalination plants this year, we will have five desalination plants. Our NEWater production capacity will also increase&nbsp;over the next five years,&nbsp;with the expansion of the Changi NEWater factory and development of Tuas Nexus.</p><p>Good water demand management is equally important in complementing efforts in water supply. PUB's water conservation efforts have shown encouraging results,&nbsp;with household water consumption decreasing to&nbsp;141 litres from 148 litres per person per day in three years. PUB will begin the deployment of 300,000 smart meters next year,&nbsp;as part of efforts to reduce water consumption&nbsp;further to 130 litres per person per day by 2030.</p><p>To inspire Singaporeans to continue efforts in water conservation,&nbsp;I am happy to launch&nbsp;the 2020 water conservation campaign. I cannot do it now like the usual way, because of COVID-19.</p><p>Today,&nbsp;PUB will kick off with a new video to remind&nbsp;Singaporeans of how far we have come in our water story.&nbsp;I feel touched and proud of our water story when I previewed the video. So will you, when you watch it tonight. It will encourage us, all Singaporeans, not to take our clean drinking water for granted.</p><p>&nbsp;Besides rising sea levels and water resilience, Mr Amrin Amin,&nbsp;Mr Liang Eng Hwa,&nbsp;Dr Chia Shi-Lu and Mr Desmond Choo have rightfully pointed out that&nbsp;warmer temperatures caused by climate change can also affect the everyday lives of Singaporeans, because it leads to an increasing mosquito population.&nbsp;In fact, warmer temperature is one of the factors that led to the surge in dengue cases last year.</p><p>Project Wolbachia is a tool which NEA is piloting to manage the mosquito population. Mr Louis Ng conveyed feedback that the&nbsp;initial surge of male Aedes mosquitoes released under Project Wolbachia causes discomfort&nbsp;to residents.</p><p>I hope to reassure residents that these male mosquitoes do not bite or transmit diseases  I say this in Parliament  and ask residents to be patient&nbsp;and bear with the initial surge. And I thank Mr Louis Ng for jointly spreading this message with us.</p><p>I am pleased to say that the Project Wolbachia trials have achieved up to 90% suppression&nbsp;of the Aedes aegypti population within the Yishun and Tampines test sites. We are considering the expansion of&nbsp;Project Wolbachia to more neighbourhoods,&nbsp;in consultation with NEA's Dengue Expert Advisory Panel.</p><p>To do this,&nbsp;NEA has set up a hi-tech mosquito farm which optimises mosquito production,&nbsp;the sorting of male mosquitoes from females and the transportation of mosquitoes for release. We are scaling up the facility's production to five million male mosquitoes a week, over the next few years,&nbsp;to battle dengue.&nbsp;This is a new and important means to future-proof ourselves against the faster breeding of mosquitoes, as a result of higher temperatures brought by climate change.</p><p>Just as NEA is establishing mosquito farms, Singapore Food Agency (SFA) is transforming food farms.&nbsp;</p><p>Food, like water,&nbsp;is a resource that can be disrupted by climate change.&nbsp;In Asia alone,&nbsp;we expect an additional 250 million mouths to feed by 2030.&nbsp;Yet,&nbsp;the amount of arable land could fall by 5% due to climate change and&nbsp;environmental degradation. Singapore,&nbsp;which currently imports more than 90% of our food, will be vulnerable.</p><p>In the face of these changes,&nbsp;we have developed three food baskets to ensure our food security by diversifying food sources,&nbsp;growing food overseas and growing local.&nbsp;We have designated 2020 as the Year of Singapore Food story&nbsp;to focus our national effort and attention.</p><p>Mr Gan Thiam Poh asked about the Government's&nbsp;success in food source diversification and&nbsp;its role in mitigating price hikes during supply&nbsp;disruptions.</p><p>Today,&nbsp;Singapore's food imports come&nbsp;from over 170 countries and regions. This did not come by chance,&nbsp;but is a result of SFA's continuous efforts. While the Government does our part,&nbsp;we require the industry to also be pro-active in diversifying supply sources&nbsp;to enhance business resiliency. For example, since last year,&nbsp;SFA has introduced requirements for egg importers&nbsp;to adopt plans to mitigate the impact of any supply disruptions.</p><p>&nbsp;With a well-diversified food supply,&nbsp;Singapore can reduce the impact&nbsp;of food supply shortages and price changes. However,&nbsp;consumers need to be mindful that the price of any particular food item&nbsp;is determined by a combination of factors, including import prices,&nbsp;exchange rates&nbsp;and profit margins which suppliers can command.</p><p>Let me elaborate on our plans to produce food locally,&nbsp;enhance food safety and encourage Singaporeans to support local produce.</p><p>Miss Cheng Li Hui and Mr Gan Thiam Poh asked about how the Government is helping&nbsp;the local agri-food industry grow. Our vision is to locally produce&nbsp;30% of Singapore's nutritional needs by 2030, but from less than 10% today.</p><p>As a small country with limited space, our land use faces many competing needs. We have dedicated less than 1% of our land for agriculture today.&nbsp;Within such tight land constraints,&nbsp;the "30 by 30" vision is highly ambitious, especially if we must achieve it within the same 1%.</p><p>Yet,&nbsp;having visited some of our new-tech farms, I am optimistic that it is achievable&nbsp;if we invest in an innovative agri-food sector.</p><p>Since 2017,&nbsp;SFA has been awarding agriculture land, through a competitive land tender basis, to agri-food companies with promising technologies.&nbsp;One such company is Green Harvest, a local vegetable farm.&nbsp;The farm uses modern greenhouse growing techniques which mitigates the impact&nbsp;of unfavourable weather conditions.&nbsp;We are helping them to adopt more technology and, with experience, we hope they can produce multiple times&nbsp;more vegetables than average.</p><p>This year, SFA will study how the larger&nbsp;Lim Chu Kang agriculture area can be planned and redeveloped to enhance our food production. This study will consider feedback from farmers&nbsp;for centralised facilities and&nbsp;services to reduce the <mark class="highlighted-text">cos</mark>t of food production.&nbsp;The study will also introduce circular economy principles,&nbsp;so that the by-product of one farm can be used&nbsp;as an input for another. SFA will work with farms,&nbsp;including successful tenders in recent&nbsp;land sales exercises,&nbsp;to work towards an exciting vision for Lim Chu Kang.</p><p>SFA's plans for Lim Chu Kang will complement&nbsp;the development of the Agri-Food Innovation Park (AFIP),&nbsp;which we announced last year as a&nbsp;high-tech agri-food cluster in Sungei Kadut.&nbsp;The food production innovations developed in AFIP can scale up in Lim Chu Kang to contribute towards our "30 by 30" vision.</p><p>&nbsp;SFA will also look to unlock the potential of farming at sea.&nbsp;Last week,&nbsp;I accompanied President Halimah to visit Eco-Ark. Eco-Ark's CEO Mr Leow Ban Tat,&nbsp;who had decades of experience in marine and&nbsp;off-shore engineering, including in Keppel, applied principles of oil rigs construction to that of a floating fish farm.&nbsp;He worked with his team to conceptualise, design and&nbsp;integrate a floating platform with&nbsp;closed-containment aquaculture technologies that treat influent sea water used to cultivate fish. These technologies mitigate the effects of pollution and algae blooms that threaten both farms and&nbsp;our marine environment.&nbsp;Ideas like this is how we will transform fish farming practices so that&nbsp;they are more productive,&nbsp;sustainable and climate-resilient.</p><p>Besides the use of technology,&nbsp;SFA will study the expansion of sustainable fish farming, where applicable, in the deeper southern waters of Singapore.&nbsp;We will ensure that such aquaculture is productive&nbsp;and environmentally responsible, such as shown by Barramundi Asia,&nbsp;Singapore's first and only deep-sea fish farm.&nbsp;SFA will work with stakeholders to study this.</p><p>We are supporting different modes of fish farming.&nbsp;Where water quality is good,&nbsp;farms can carry out deep sea farming.&nbsp;Where it is not, farms can adopt closed-containment technologies.&nbsp;Where there can be synergies with land-based water and waste systems,&nbsp;land-based aquaculture can be conducted.</p><p>Like land,&nbsp;every space at sea can be used for food production but must be judiciously managed.</p><h6>3.00 pm</h6><p>Mr Gan Thiam Poh also asked how the Government is helping local farms remain competitive.&nbsp;Over the years,&nbsp;our economic agencies have been supporting local farms in their overseas expansion. Growing their businesses overseas allow farms to tap on land and manpower,&nbsp;lower production <mark class="highlighted-text">cos</mark>ts,&nbsp;and break into new markets.</p><p>I encourage our farms to take a leaf from the food manufacturing industry,&nbsp;where companies like Tee Yih Jia Food anchor&nbsp;their expertise locally while expanding operations overseas.&nbsp;Some of our farms,&nbsp;like Apollo,&nbsp;have already done so.</p><p>These food products produced overseas, while not "Made in Singapore",&nbsp;should be marketed as "Made by Singapore",&nbsp;since they uphold the same reputation&nbsp;for high quality and standards as those produced domestically.</p><p>To complement these efforts, SFA,&nbsp;Enterprise Singapore and the industry will be developing Clean-Green Standards for urban farms which harness resource-efficient technologies to produce food in cleaner environments.&nbsp;They will study ways to incorporate information that&nbsp;more people are concerned about, such as the carbon footprint of food they produce.&nbsp;The standards will serve as a mark of quality assurance that our farms can use to differentiate their products in the market.</p><p>Let me move on to incentives and funding.&nbsp;Over the past five years,&nbsp;SFA has committed $38 million&nbsp;from the Agriculture Productivity Fund, or APF, to support more than 100 farms in their productivity-enhancing investments.&nbsp;I am happy to share that the APF&nbsp;will be extended till the end of 2020. Beyond this,&nbsp;we will review how we can expand our support for technology test-bedding,&nbsp;adoption and resource efficiency in our farms.</p><p>New innovations to produce food more productively and sustainably are also needed.&nbsp;The first research grant call launched by SFA and A*STAR last December under the $144 million Singapore Food Story R&amp;D Programme&nbsp;will facilitate this.</p><p>One theme under this research programme is the production of novel foods,&nbsp;like alternative proteins.&nbsp;Traditional meat production methods are often land and water intensive,&nbsp;and generate high levels of emissions.&nbsp;Today,&nbsp;alternative proteins,&nbsp;such as Impossible and Quorn, are poised to become game-changers.&nbsp;I hope Members will enjoy the alternative protein food items from Tee Yih Jia Food and Empress served at today's tea break after our speeches.</p><p>Miss Cheng Li Hui asked about food safety of novel foods.&nbsp;We will ensure that only novel foods that are&nbsp;safe for consumption are introduced in Singapore. Last year,&nbsp;SFA implemented a new regulatory framework to require companies to seek its approval and undergo a scientific pre-market assessment before placing novel foods in the market.&nbsp;This framework facilitates the sale of alternative proteins in Singapore while ensuring their safety. Companies,&nbsp;such as local start-up Shiok Meats,&nbsp;who is looking to produce cell-cultured shrimp, will be able to benefit from this framework.</p><p>To support the assessment of novel food, SFA will establish&nbsp;an international expert working group to provide scientific advice&nbsp;on food safety.</p><p>Beyond food supply,&nbsp;we must not also forget our fundamental mission, that is to ensure that the food we eat is safe. SFA takes a serious approach towards upholding high food safety and hygiene standards.&nbsp;Since its formation,&nbsp;SFA has conducted more than 5,200 inspections in 2019 and taken more than 1,600 enforcement actions against errant operators.</p><p>SFA will be introducing new measures to safeguard food safety.&nbsp;Premises which pose higher food safety risks,&nbsp;such as caterers and central kitchens, will be required to install&nbsp;closed-circuit television cameras.&nbsp;This enables them to monitor food safety performance and&nbsp;provide evidence in the event of food safety&nbsp;allegations.</p><p>Enhanced measures will also be targeted at businesses that cater to vulnerable groups. Food businesses catering to preschools, kindergartens and&nbsp;nursing homes will be required to maintain&nbsp;food retention samples,&nbsp;which will facilitate investigations during food-borne outbreaks.</p><p>&nbsp;From 2021,&nbsp;SFA will also introduce a single unified framework to license and recognise all food establishments.&nbsp;SFA will award a longer licence duration and a higher recognition to food establishments which uphold high food safety and&nbsp;hygiene standards. This way,&nbsp;SFA can re-prioritise resources to focus on checks to ensure compliance with food safety requirements.</p><p>Mr Louis Ng asked about the&nbsp;introduction of a Good Samaritan Food Donation Act.&nbsp;</p><p>MEWR is currently working on a legislation to enhance our food safety regime.&nbsp;As part of this,&nbsp;we are looking to introduce a legislation to facilitate food donation.&nbsp;</p><p>Any legislation will need to strike a balance between&nbsp;facilitating food donation and ensuring food safety.&nbsp;We will be happy to work with Mr Louis Ng.</p><p>Mr Chairman, let me conclude my last section of my speech by touching on the role of all Singaporeans in taking climate action.&nbsp;As the Government,&nbsp;we are providing leadership in tackling climate change.&nbsp;We do this by enhancing our climate pledge under&nbsp;the Paris Agreement and&nbsp;working with the industry to guide their transformation to a low-carbon economy.&nbsp;While the Government can lead on sustainability,&nbsp;the people must champion its cause.</p><p>For instance,&nbsp;Singaporeans can make conscious climate-friendly choices in our daily essentials,&nbsp;such as food.&nbsp;Locally-produced food is of high quality, incurs less carbon miles,&nbsp;lasts longer and tastes good.&nbsp;By eating local,&nbsp;Singaporeans are making an environmentally sustainable choice.&nbsp;In the process,&nbsp;we support our farmers by choosing food that is grown in Singapore.</p><p>Since 2017,&nbsp;SFA has partnered Singapore Agro-Food Enterprises Federation (SAFEF) to organise&nbsp;farmers' markets to raise awareness of local produce.&nbsp;Last month,&nbsp;I unveiled a new logo which Singapore farmers can soon use to help Singaporeans identify local produce.</p><p>The community can also support local production by participating in community farming projects. For example,&nbsp;nearly 80% of NParks' Community in Bloom gardens&nbsp;grow edibles.&nbsp;</p><p>There is also potential for companies and the community to engage in commercial urban farming that involves the community.&nbsp;Since the launch of the pilot rooftop farm, Citiponics in Ang Mo Kio,&nbsp;interest from the public  as Er Dr Lee Bee Wah mentioned just now  has increased towards urban farming&nbsp;in community spaces.&nbsp;We will provide more opportunities for urban rooftop farming.&nbsp;In the coming months,&nbsp;SFA will tender 16 rooftop spaces on&nbsp;HDB multi-storey car parks across the island, totalling over 30,000 sqm.&nbsp;</p><p>As we grow our agri-sector, let us get involved in&nbsp;growing food in our own backyards.</p><p>Now, let us talk about electrical appliances.&nbsp;Take refrigerators.&nbsp;They are amongst the top three highest&nbsp;energy-consuming household appliances today. However,&nbsp;many households&nbsp;continue to purchase 1 to 2 ticks refrigerators, partly due to the higher upfront <mark class="highlighted-text">cos</mark>ts of energy-efficient models,&nbsp;without considering the savings&nbsp;from electricity consumption over the long term. Similarly,&nbsp;showering takes up a significant proportion of water consumption,&nbsp;yet many households are choosing the less water-efficient fittings.</p><p>To help households living in 1- to 3-room HDB flats invest in energy and water-efficient models, the Government will introduce a $24.8 million Climate-Friendly Household Package.&nbsp;These households will receive a once-off&nbsp;$150 voucher to purchase refrigerator models approved by NEA that have 3 ticks and above. Not only will this help fight climate change, households can benefit from electricity <mark class="highlighted-text">cos</mark>t savings of up to $60 annually.</p><p>We will also extend the&nbsp;"Switch and Save  use LED" programme launched in 2018 to all 3-room HDB flats. In addition,&nbsp;we will give all 1 to 3-room HDB households a&nbsp;$50 voucher to offset the <mark class="highlighted-text">cos</mark>t of changing their shower fittings to more water-efficient 3-tick models.</p><p>If all eligible households make the switch,&nbsp;we can collectively reduce emissions equivalent to taking around 12,600 cars off the road and save up to 400 million gallons of water&nbsp;annually.</p><p>Next,&nbsp;let me focus on the ground-up efforts of our youths and citizens to address climate change.</p><p>I commend our youths who are channelling&nbsp;their passions into positive change for the environment.&nbsp;During the Year of Climate Action in 2018, more than two climate-related activities per day were conducted,&nbsp;and youths played a big part in them.&nbsp;Recently,&nbsp;a group of 50 youths participated in the Environmental Track of the Youth Action Challenge organised by MCCY,&nbsp;submitting projects ranging from waste reduction to&nbsp;green technologies.&nbsp;</p><p>We work together with these youths to deliver some of these ideas into reality.&nbsp;To empower our youths with knowledge and experience on sustainability,&nbsp;my Ministry is working with MOE and the National Youth Council (NYC).&nbsp;We want to introduce more sustainability&nbsp;programmes in schools and community facilities.&nbsp;Even Outward Bound Singapore (OBS) @ Coney will be designed to be a&nbsp;eco-experience for our youths to learn and enjoy.</p><p>The Government will work with schools to introduce initiatives&nbsp;which will integrate green infrastructure,&nbsp;sustainability programmes and curriculum design to inculcate positive environmental values in our youths,&nbsp;like ones done in Mee Toh School.</p><p>Another group of Singaporeans whose efforts&nbsp;I would like to recognise are those that are willing to share their experiences and ideas&nbsp;on sustainability.</p><p>Last year,&nbsp;my Ministry convened our first Citizens' Workgroup on recycling, where 48 participants joined us&nbsp;on a month-long journey of co-creation.&nbsp;We will be setting up two more Citizens' Workgroups this year, focusing on more issues that Singaporeans care&nbsp;about.</p><p>One Workgroup will start the conversation on how we can increase demand for local produce.&nbsp;I invite interested members of the public to visit
